# Anchor Interview

## Summary

This session began with a comprehensive interview to improve the implementation plan, leading to a major renderer decision, followed by consistency fixes and an operator redesign.

**Implementation Plan Interview:**
- Conducted detailed interview using AskUserQuestion tool covering all engine requirements
- Software renderer motivations discussed: aesthetic, simplicity, web reliability
- Key discoveries that changed the calculus:
  - Discrete rotation was only "acceptable tradeoff" ÔÇö not a desired aesthetic
  - Additive blending is essential (explosions, energy effects)
  - Console targets (Switch, Xbox, PlayStation) require GPU APIs ÔÇö incompatible with software rendering
  - You already know GLSL and have written 500+ line shaders; the concern was preference, not capability
  - Pixel control examples were mostly theoretical (Noita-style) rather than needed for ball combat games
- Audio gap identified: SDL_mixer insufficient (no pitch shifting), needs research (miniaudio vs SoLoud)
- Physics confirmed: Box2D 3.x, fixed 60fps timestep for determinism
- Platform targets: Windows, Linux, Web primary; mobile/console if games succeed
- Debug workflow: print debugging + error screen sufficient
- C structure: single `anchor.c`, resources live forever, single executable distribution
- Distant artistic vision revealed: "7 stories that are 7 games" with merged game/narrative elements
- **Final decision: OpenGL** ÔÇö clean decision with no "software-like mental model" compromise

**Documentation Updates:**
- Created new `ANCHOR_IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md` with OpenGL, 10 phases, TBD audio
- Deleted old `IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md` (software renderer based)
- Updated ANCHOR.md: removed software renderer sections (~200 lines), added OpenGL rendering
- Updated CLAUDE.md: changed references from software rendering to OpenGL

**Consistency Fixes (multiple rounds, including pre-compaction work):**
- Timer architecture: child object with `after`, `every`, `tween` methods via `self.timer:after(...)`
- Engine object pattern: timer/spring/collider are tree objects named after themselves by default
- Spring examples: single springs use `spring(1, 200, 10)` Ôćĺ `self.spring`; multiples use explicit names
- Collider naming: optional name parameter separate from physics tag
- Color format: `an.colors.white` without `[0]` indexing
- Platforms: Linux as primary alongside Windows and Web
- Changed `add_child` Ôćĺ `add` in reference implementation
- Build function example: `self:add(timer())` Ôćĺ `self + timer()`

**High-Level Engine Analysis:**
- Confirmed locality principle genuinely achieved through operator pipeline
- Confirmed no-bureaucracy goal achieved via globals and automatic cleanup
- Tree-based ownership identified as core elegant insight
- Collision handling and draw order noted as necessary tradeoffs that break locality
- Critiqued `%` and `//` operators as arbitrary with no mnemonic ÔÇö led to redesign

**Operator Redesign:**
- Replaced arbitrary `%` (early) and `//` (late) with unified `/` operator using phase helpers
- Explored multiple alias combinations: A/E (A conflicted with `all()`), Y/E, T/E, X/N
- **Final aliases:** E (object/Entity), U (early/Under), L (late/Late), X (named/eXplicit), A (all/All)
- All runic (angular, symmetrical letters), all with decent mnemonics
- `/` operator detects `U`, `L`, and `X` helpers via `__early`, `__late`, or string key patterns
- Helper signatures support both forms: `U(fn)` and `U('name', fn)`
- Updated all three documentation files with new operator syntax

**Final Cleanup:**
- Removed all mentions of deprecated `%` and `//` operators
- Fixed simplified alias definitions to show full signatures

**Files modified:**
- Created: `docs/ANCHOR_IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md`
- Deleted: `docs/IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md`
- Updated: `docs/ANCHOR.md`, `.claude/CLAUDE.md`
- Created: `yue/examples.yue` (renamed from `operator_comparison.yue`)

---

> Hi, Claude, do you know the AskUserQuestionTool?

~~~
Yes, I'm familiar with the AskUserQuestion tool. It's one of my available tools that allows me to ask you questions during our conversation when I need clarification, want to validate assumptions, or need help making decisions.

**How it works:**

- I can ask 1-4 questions at once
- Each question has a short header (max 12 chars) displayed as a chip/tag
- I provide 2-4 options per question, each with a label and description
- You can always select "Other" to provide custom text input
- There's a `multiSelect` option for questions where multiple answers apply

**When I'd use it:**

- Gathering your preferences or requirements
- Clarifying ambiguous instructions
- Getting decisions on implementation choices as I work
- Offering choices about which direction to take

**Example scenario:**

If you asked me to "add authentication to the app," I might use AskUserQuestion to ask:

```
Question: "Which authentication method should we use?"
Header: "Auth method"
Options:
  - JWT tokens (stateless, good for APIs)
  - Session cookies (traditional, simpler for web apps)
  - OAuth2 (delegate to external providers)
```

It's particularly useful for the kinds of architectural and implementation decisions that come up when working on Anchor or games built with it ÔÇö where you have specific opinions and I shouldn't just guess.

Is there something specific you'd like me to ask you about, or were you curious about how it functions?
~~~

> Ask me the same questions again, but before that, tell me if the questions are multi-choice or only one. In the first question, the first 3 answers applied, but I didn't know if pressing enter on one answer would make it the only answer or not.

Good catch ÔÇö I should have been clearer. Here's how it works:

**Single-select (`multiSelect: false`)**: You can only pick ONE answer. Pressing enter submits that one choice. You can also select "Other" to type a custom response.

**Multi-select (`multiSelect: true`)**: You can select MULTIPLE answers before submitting. This is better for questions where several options might apply simultaneously.

All four of my previous questions were single-select, which was wrong for the first one since your motivations overlap. Let me re-ask with better settings:

Very illuminating answers. Some important shifts:

1. **Discrete rotation is a tradeoff, not a goal** ÔÇö this actually weakens the software renderer case since OpenGL could give smooth rotation with the same chunky pixel aesthetic (nearest-neighbor filtering at 480├Ś270).

2. **Additive blending is essential** ÔÇö doable in software but trivial in OpenGL (`glBlendFunc`). This is a point in OpenGL's favor.

3. **Minimal animation** ÔÇö good, no complex animation system needed.

4. **Varies wildly on physics bodies** ÔÇö this makes performance harder to predict.

This round revealed something critical: **Consoles are a target.** 

This is a major factor for the renderer decision. Console platforms (Switch, Xbox, PlayStation) essentially require GPU-based rendering ÔÇö you don't get direct framebuffer access like software rendering assumes. Software rendering would be a dead end for console ports.

Combined with:
- Discrete rotations being unwanted (OpenGL gives smooth)
- Additive blending being essential (trivial in OpenGL)
- "All of the above" chaos (performance matters)
- Shader complexity being a concern (solvable with a thin abstraction)

The case for OpenGL is strengthening significantly. Let me explore this more, plus other areas:

<details>
<summary><code>AskUserQuestion </code></summary>

<pre><code>User has answered your questions: "Console targets change everything. Were you aware that software rendering is essentially incompatible with console development? Consoles require GPU APIs (DirectX on Xbox, GNM on PlayStation, NVN/Vulkan on Switch)."="This is new information that changes my thinking, but also consoles are only a goal if a game is very successful. In that case, taking the time to also use GPU APIs would be worth it and it wouldn't bother me. In that case, I'd have both software renderer and GPU going on. But then whatever we make use of in the software renderer that can't be easily translated to the traditional GPU APIs (which is one of the reasons why we'd be using such a renderer in the first place) would be hard to do... So I don't know yet.", "For shader complexity concerns: L├ľVE (which you used before) hides shaders behind a simple API but uses OpenGL underneath. Would a similar thin abstraction satisfy you? You'd write `layer:circle()` not `glDrawArrays`."="Yes, such abstraction works, but the underlying code still needs to be written by me (or you). Even then, controlling pixels individually with the software renderer is much more freeing than being bound by shaders, which make such control a lot harder. WebGL 2.0 has some Safari quirks but WebGL 1.0 is solid. Does knowing this change your concern level?"="I understand that I can have my code working across pretty much most browsers depending on how I choose to do it, so it wasn't a big concern.", "Local multiplayer (same screen, multiple controllers) - is this ever a goal?"="Online only". You can now continue with the user's answers in mind.</code></pre>

</details>

Now we're getting to the heart of it. The core tension isn't really about complexity or reliability ÔÇö it's about **creative freedom**:

> "Controlling pixels individually with the software renderer is much more freeing than being bound by shaders... having complete control over individual pixels feels much more intuitive than writing effects using shaders."

This is a legitimate creative preference. The question becomes: what specific effects need that pixel-level control, and is there a way to get that freedom while keeping console viability?

<details>
<summary><code>AskUserQuestion </code></summary>

<pre><code>User has answered your questions: "Can you describe 1-2 specific visual effects where pixel-level control feels essential? I want to understand concrete examples, not abstract preferences. This is very honest and helpful. The picture is clarifying:

1. **Concrete examples are limited** ÔÇö pixel sorting is valid, Noita-style simulation is valid, but you acknowledge not having many concrete cases. The preference is partly creative intuition about "space of possibilities."

2. **Performance is untested** ÔÇö assuming 480├Ś270 + 200 sprites + alpha blending works at 60fps without measurement is risky.

3. **Open to hybrid** ÔÇö worth exploring.

Here's my observation: SNKRX and BYTEPATH (your shipped games) don't use pixel-level effects. They use circles, shapes, physics, screen shake, particles. These don't require per-pixel control. The Noita example is a *different kind of game* than your typical ball combat games.

Let me explore this tension more, plus move into other spec areas:

This round significantly clarifies the situation:

1. **Near-term games are ball combat, not pixel simulation** ÔÇö the Noita-style pixel freedom is theoretical for now, not immediate.

2. **Backup plan exists** ÔÇö if software rendering is slow, switch to GPU. Pragmatic.

3. **Text effects are sophisticated** ÔÇö but L├ľVE does these with GPU rendering, so text effects don't require software rendering.

4. **Emoji art style** ÔÇö distinctive and interesting! Just PNG sprites, works with any renderer.

**My assessment so far:** Software rendering is defensible for the near-term (ball combat games at 480├Ś270), with GPU as the backup plan. The pixel-freedom argument is more about future potential than immediate needs. The console concern is deferred to "if successful, rewrite."

Important findings:

1. **Audio needs pitch shifting immediately** ÔÇö SDL_mixer doesn't handle this well. This is a gap in the current plan. Alternatives:
   - **miniaudio**: Single-header, supports pitch, modern API
   - **SoLoud**: Game audio library, pitch shifting, effects, mixing
   - Manual pitch implementation on top of SDL_mixer (complex)

2. **Debug workflow**: Print + error screen. Simple, no hot reload needed.

3. **Resolution**: Per-game configurable.

4. **Fullscreen toggle**: Essential.

3. **Web assets**: Preload all ÔÇö simple.

4. **Custom shaders if OpenGL**: Want them from start ÔÇö interesting! This shows willingness to learn GLSL if needed, which somewhat contradicts the shader complexity concern.

Let me explore the website rendering goal and a few final areas:

This is incredibly valuable context:

1. **Website rendering**: Distant goal, but the artistic vision is beautiful ÔÇö "7 stories that are also 7 games, with merged game elements." This needs pixel control HTML can't provide. Deferred but shapes long-term design.

2. **GLSL experience**: You already know GLSL and have written 500+ line shaders! The concern is about preference ("don't like it, not intuitive"), not capability. With my help, you could manage it.

3. **Why engine**: Full control + specific features. Ownership matters.

This changes the renderer calculus: you *can* write shaders if needed, you just prefer not to. Software rendering aligns with preference; OpenGL is within capability.

Let me ask a few final technical questions, then I'll synthesize everything into the new implementation plan:

<details>
<summary><code>AskUserQuestion </code></summary>

<pre><code>User has answered your questions: "Threading: should the engine be single-threaded (simpler) or have audio/physics on separate threads (better performance, more complex)?"="This is a detail that I'd hope you'd solve, I don't know enough about this to tell.", "Memory/resource management: when sprites/sounds/fonts are loaded, do they stay forever (simple) or need explicit unloading (more control)?"="Live forever", "C code style: do you have a preference for how C code is structured? (single-file vs many files, naming conventions, etc.)"="Single anchor.c", "Distribution: when shipping a game, how should it be packaged?"="Single executable". You can now continue with the user's answers in mind.</code></pre>

</details>

Excellent. Final answers:

1. **Threading**: I'll recommend single-threaded with SDL handling audio callbacks (which naturally runs separately).
2. **Resources**: Live forever ÔÇö simple.
3. **C style**: Single `anchor.c` ÔÇö monolithic, easier to navigate.
4. **Distribution**: Single executable with embedded assets.

I have a comprehensive picture now. Let me ask just a couple more clarifying questions before I write the new implementation plan:

Let's have that discussion about the renderer. I'll lay out my analysis based on everything you've shared.

---

## The Renderer Decision: Software vs. OpenGL

### What You Want (distilled from our conversation)

1. **Pixel-level control** feels more intuitive than shaders for effects
2. **Additive blending** is essential (explosions, energy effects)
3. **Smooth rotation** would be preferred over discrete angles (if easy)
4. **Chunky pixel aesthetic** at 480├Ś270 or similar
5. **Simplicity** ÔÇö don't want to fight GPU state management
6. **Web deployment** works reliably
7. **Console-compatible** if a game succeeds (distant concern)
8. **Custom shaders** available if you go OpenGL route (you know GLSL, just don't love it)

### Software Renderer: Honest Assessment

**Pros:**
- Direct pixel manipulation ÔÇö iterate pixels, apply any math
- No GPU state to manage, no driver bugs
- Intuitive for effects like pixel sorting
- Single code path for all platforms
- Debugging is just "look at the pixel array"

**Cons:**
- **Performance is unverified** at your scale (200+ sprites, alpha blending, additive blending)
- **Additive blending** requires a separate blend loop per pixel (doable but adds cost)
- **Smooth rotation** requires per-pixel sampling (expensive) or precomputed frames (memory)
- **Console-incompatible** ÔÇö would need complete rewrite for GPU
- **Scaling limits** ÔÇö if a game idea needs 500 particles, you might hit a wall

**The core risk:** You're betting that 480├Ś270 is small enough that brute-force software rendering stays fast. This might be true! But you haven't tested it, and "I'll switch to GPU if it's slow" is a significant pivot mid-development.

### OpenGL: Honest Assessment

**Pros:**
- **Smooth rotation** is free (just change the angle uniform)
- **Additive blending** is one line: `glBlendFunc(GL_SRC_ALPHA, GL_ONE)`
- **Performance headroom** ÔÇö GPU handles thousands of sprites trivially
- **Console-portable** ÔÇö same mental model, different backend API
- **Custom shaders** give pixel-level control *when you need it*
- You already know GLSL (500+ line shaders)

**Cons:**
- **State management complexity** ÔÇö glEnable/glDisable, binding order
- **Shader debugging** is painful compared to stepping through pixel loops
- **You don't enjoy it** ÔÇö this is real and matters for motivation
- **WebGL quirks** ÔÇö though WebGL 1.0 is quite stable now

**The key insight:** With a thin abstraction layer, you'd write `layer:circle()` not `glDrawArrays()`. The shader complexity is hidden until you need custom effects. And when you *do* need custom effects, shaders give you per-pixel control ÔÇö just expressed differently.

### My Recommendation

**Start with OpenGL, but with a software-renderer-like mental model.**

Here's why:

1. **You can get pixel control via shaders.** Pixel sorting? Write a pixel sort shader. Per-pixel color manipulation? Fragment shader. The operations are the same, just expressed in GLSL instead of a loop. With my help, we can write any pixel effect you can describe.

2. **Your real concern is intuition, not capability.** You *know* GLSL. You just find it unintuitive. But with me as a collaborator, you can describe the effect you want, and I translate it to GLSL. Your intuition drives, I handle the syntax.

3. **The backup plan is risky.** "Switch to GPU if slow" sounds easy but it's a significant rewrite. Starting with GPU means the backup is "optimize the shaders" ÔÇö a much smaller change.

4. **Additive blending + smooth rotation + performance** all come free with OpenGL. These aren't minor conveniences ÔÇö they're things you said you want.

5. **The chunky pixel aesthetic is preserved.** Render to a 480├Ś270 framebuffer with nearest-neighbor filtering. The pixels stay chunky. This isn't a tradeoff.

### The Counter-Argument (Why Software Might Still Be Right)

If your games stay at SNKRX scale (50-100 objects, simple effects), software rendering at 480├Ś270 is probably fine. The pixel control intuition matters for creative flow. And you've shipped games before ÔÇö you know what you actually use.

The honest truth: **SNKRX and BYTEPATH don't use effects that require pixel-level control.** Circles, shapes, screen shake, particles ÔÇö all GPU-friendly. The pixel control argument is about *potential* effects you *might* want, not effects you've actually needed.

---

**My question for you:** Is the pixel control preference based on effects you've actually wanted but couldn't achieve in L├ľVE? Or is it more about creative possibility space ÔÇö wanting the freedom even if you haven't used it?

This might determine the answer.

> OK, let's just go with OpenGL then, and not even keep a "software-renderer-like" mental model.

Good. Clean decision. OpenGL it is.

**Major changes from the original plan:**
- **OpenGL renderer** instead of software rendering
- **Smooth rotation** is now free (shader-based)
- **Additive blending** is a one-line API call
- **Custom shaders** are available from the start
- **Audio library is TBD** ÔÇö flagged for research (miniaudio vs SoLoud) since SDL_mixer doesn't support pitch shifting
- **Web build is Phase 2** (right after skeleton) to catch platform issues early
- **Effects phase is separate** from rendering, focused on post-processing shaders
- **Cleaner phase organization** ÔÇö 10 phases total, each with clear deliverable

**Key decisions captured:**
- Single `anchor.c` file
- Resources live forever (no unloading)
- Fixed timestep for determinism
- Per-game configurable resolution
- Single executable distribution with embedded assets

**Deferred items explicitly listed:**
- Gamepad, Steam Input
- Hot reload, debug tools
- Save/load, networking
- Full UI system

  1373Ôćĺ
  1374ÔćĺEach layer is a framebuffer. Draw commands are batched per layer and flushed at end of frame:
  1375Ôćĺ
  1376Ôćĺ1. Lua calls `layer:circle()`, `layer:draw_image()`, etc.
  1377Ôćĺ2. Each call appends to that layer's command buffer
  1378Ôćĺ3. At end of frame, C processes all commands in a tight loop
  1379Ôćĺ4. Effects are applied per-layer (C functions, configured from Lua)
  1380Ôćĺ5. Layers composite to screen via alpha blending
  1381Ôćĺ
  1382Ôćĺ### Sprites
  1383Ôćĺ
  1384ÔćĺSprites use precomputed rotations (16 or 32 angles) with runtime scaling. This trades memory for computation and creates a distinctive aesthetic with discrete rotation angles.
  1385Ôćĺ
  1386Ôćĺ### Effects
  1387Ôćĺ
  1388ÔćĺPost-processing effects are implemented in C and configured from Lua:
  1389Ôćĺ

  1432Ôćĺ
  1433Ôćĺ**Batch Rendering:** Geometry is accumulated into vertex buffers and drawn in batches to minimize draw calls. Batch breaks occur on texture/shader/blend mode changes.

  1562Ôćĺ7. **Physics:** Box2D 3.1 wrapper with sensor/contact events and queries
  1563Ôćĺ8. **Random:** Deterministic PRNG
  1564Ôćĺ9. **Text:** TTF font loading and glyph rendering
  1565Ôćĺ10. **Object system:** Tree with immediate kill propagation (YueScript)
  1566Ôćĺ11. **Timer/Spring/Collider:** As child objects
  1567Ôćĺ12. **Error handling:** Protected calls, error screen
  1568Ôćĺ
  1569ÔćĺEach step testable independently.
  1570Ôćĺ
  1571Ôćĺ---
  1572Ôćĺ
  1573Ôćĺ## Deferred Features</code></pre>

</details>

<pre><code>1Ôćĺ# Anchor Implementation Plan
     2Ôćĺ
     3ÔćĺC engine with YueScript scripting, OpenGL rendering, targeting Windows and Web.
     4Ôćĺ
     5Ôćĺ---
     6Ôćĺ
     7Ôćĺ## Summary of Key Decisions
     8Ôćĺ
     9Ôćĺ| Area | Decision | Rationale |
    10Ôćĺ|------|----------|-----------|
    11Ôćĺ| Renderer | OpenGL | Smooth rotation, additive blending, performance headroom, console-portable |
    12Ôćĺ| Audio | TBD (miniaudio or SoLoud) | Need pitch shifting; SDL_mixer insufficient |
    13Ôćĺ| Physics | Box2D 3.1 | Already used, true ball-to-ball collisions needed |
    14Ôćĺ| Scripting | Lua 5.4 + YueScript | Build-time compilation with `-r` flag for line numbers |
    15Ôćĺ| Timestep | Fixed 60fps | Determinism matters for potential replay support |
    16Ôćĺ| Resolution | Per-game configurable | 480├Ś270, 640├Ś360, or custom; integer scaling |
    17Ôćĺ| C Structure | Single anchor.c | Monolithic file, easier navigation |
    18Ôćĺ| Resources | Live forever | Games are small enough; no unloading needed |
    19Ôćĺ| Distribution | Single executable | Assets embedded |
    20Ôćĺ
    21Ôćĺ---
    22Ôćĺ
    23Ôćĺ## Build Strategy

I've read all three files. Here's my analysis of issues found:

## Issues Found

### 1. Contradictions Between Files

**1.1 Pitch shifting in Deferred Features (ANCHOR.md:1468)**
```
- Advanced audio (pitch shifting, positional)
```
But the implementation plan says pitch shifting is **essential** and needed immediately. This contradicts our interview where you said pitch shifting is needed right away.

**1.2 File Structure contradiction (ANCHOR.md:1409-1415)**
Shows multiple C files (`main.c`, `an_render.c`, `an_physics.c`, etc.) but the implementation plan explicitly says "Single anchor.c" and the key decisions table confirms this.

**1.3 CLAUDE.md references removed section**
Line 10 says to read ANCHOR.md for "The mental model (engine services vs object tree)" but we just removed the Engine Services section.

**1.4 Sound API inconsistency**
CLAUDE.md line 247: `an:sound_play(bounce_sound, speed / 100)` (number as second arg)
Implementation plan: `an:sound_play(hit, {volume = 0.5, pitch = 1.2})` (table with named params)

**1.5 Rectangle API inconsistency**
ANCHOR.md: `game:rectangle(x, y, w, h, rx, ry, color)` (with rounded corners?)
Implementation plan: `game:rectangle(x, y, w, h, color)` (no rounded corners)

### 2. Redundancy

**2.1 Rendering Architecture + OpenGL Implementation Notes overlap**
Both sections describe layers as framebuffers, batch rendering, frame flow, blend modes. Should consolidate.

**2.2 Migration Path duplicates Phase Summary**
ANCHOR.md has a 12-step migration path that's essentially the same as the implementation plan's phases.

**2.3 Build System and Resolution sections are too sparse**
One or two lines each, adding no value beyond the implementation plan.

### 3. Unclear/Awkward Design

**3.1 `mouse_world_position(camera)` in implementation plan**
Suggests C knows about cameras, but camera is supposed to be pure YueScript. Should be `camera:screen_to_world(mx, my)` instead.

**3.2 `an.colors.white[0]` in examples**
Used in several places but never documented. Is there a built-in color palette?

**3.3 Timer initialization confusion**
ANCHOR.md shows `self:timer()` as a method to "enable timer functionality", but the object class code shows timers as internal arrays. Is there a timer child object or just an array?

**3.4 YueScript directory structure**
Implementation plan shows `timer.yue`, `spring.yue`, `collider.yue` as separate files, but ANCHOR.md shows collider as a simple factory function. Are these separate files or part of object.yue?

  1251Ôćĺ

  1253Ôćĺ
  1254Ôćĺ## Rendering
  1255Ôćĺ
  1256ÔćĺAnchor uses OpenGL 3.3 Core Profile (WebGL 2.0 compatible) for rendering, targeting 480├Ś270 or 640├Ś360 base resolution (per-game configurable). The low resolution is rendered to a framebuffer texture, then integer-scaled to the window with nearest-neighbor filtering for crisp pixels.
  1257Ôćĺ
  1258Ôćĺ### Layers
  1259Ôćĺ
  1260ÔćĺEach layer is a framebuffer object (FBO). Draw commands are batched per layer:
  1261Ôćĺ
  1262Ôćĺ1. Lua calls `layer:circle()`, `layer:draw_image()`, etc.
  1263Ôćĺ2. Geometry is batched into vertex buffers (batch breaks on texture/shader/blend mode changes)
  1264Ôćĺ3. At end of frame, layers are rendered via draw calls
  1265Ôćĺ4. Post-processing shaders are applied per-layer
  1266Ôćĺ5. Layers composite to screen in defined order
  1267Ôćĺ
  1268Ôćĺ```lua
  1269Ôćĺgame = an:layer('game')
  1270Ôćĺeffects = an:layer('effects')
  1271Ôćĺui = an:layer('ui')
  1272Ôćĺ
  1273Ôćĺgame:circle(x, y, radius, color)
  1274Ôćĺgame:rectangle(x, y, w, h, color)
  1275Ôćĺgame:line(x1, y1, x2, y2, color, width)
  1276Ôćĺgame:draw_image(img, x, y, r, sx, sy, ox, oy, color)
  1277Ôćĺ```

     2Ôćĺ
     3ÔćĺC engine with YueScript scripting, OpenGL rendering, targeting Windows and Web.
     4Ôćĺ
     5Ôćĺ---
     6Ôćĺ
     7Ôćĺ## Summary of Key Decisions
     8Ôćĺ
     9Ôćĺ| Area | Decision | Rationale |
    10Ôćĺ|------|----------|-----------|
    11Ôćĺ| Renderer | OpenGL | Smooth rotation, additive blending, performance headroom, console-portable |
    12Ôćĺ| Audio | TBD (miniaudio or SoLoud) | Need pitch shifting; SDL_mixer insufficient |
    13Ôćĺ| Physics | Box2D 3.1 | Already used, true ball-to-ball collisions needed |
    14Ôćĺ| Scripting | Lua 5.4 + YueScript | Build-time compilation with `-r` flag for line numbers |
    15Ôćĺ| Timestep | Fixed 60fps | Determinism matters for potential replay support |
    16Ôćĺ| Resolution | Per-game configurable | 480├Ś270, 640├Ś360, or custom; integer scaling |
    17Ôćĺ| C Structure | Single anchor.c | Monolithic file, easier navigation |

## Questions for You

<details>
<summary><code>AskUserQuestion </code></summary>

<pre><code>User has answered your questions: "Color indices use [0] as base (an.colors.white[0]), but Lua tables are 1-indexed. Is this intentional C-style indexing, or should it be [1] as base with negatives darker and positives lighter?"="This was how I did colors in Anchor so far, but I think it's easier to just have direct colors and leave darker/brighter values for later. So just assume a default palette exists (an.colors.white, etc), and that colors themselves passed to functions follow whatever convention this is (is it a Lua table? C struct pointer? I don't know, but it doesn't matter API-wise)", "ANCHOR.md Drawing example uses self.body:get_position() but Collider section creates self.collider. Which name should the collider use?"="All mixin-like objects, timers, springs, colliders, and more that I ended up not mentioning, are not objects that live as children of their parents. So if the collider object has a body attribute, and you want to access that from the player, and the collider on the player is on the .collider attribute, it'd be self.collider.body. However, the collider exposes :get_position such that the parent doesn't have to access the internal attributes, so it'd be, from the parent, self.collider:get_position(). The one change we made to the engine is that EVERY object is a part of the tree now, so that's how it goes.", "Objects can have special methods (update:, late_update:) AND actions (/ operator). If both exist, what happens?"="Both run", "Timers are described as 'child objects' but stored in @timers array, not @children. Which is the true model?"="Could you show me the example exactly? They're supposed to be child objects, I don't remember where a timer arrays exists.". You can now continue with the user's answers in mind.</code></pre>

</details>

~~~
Thanks for the clarifications! Let me show you the timer array issue and ask the remaining questions:

**Timer array location** - In ANCHOR.md lines 1036-1059, the YueScript class implementation shows:

```yuescript
after: (delay, name_or_fn, fn) =>
  @\timer! unless @timers
  if type(name_or_fn) == 'string'
    ...
    table.insert @timers, timer_obj
  else
    table.insert @timers, {delay: delay, elapsed: 0, fn: name_or_fn}
```

This uses `@timers` as an array, not `@children`. If timers should be child objects, this reference implementation needs to change.
~~~

<details>
<summary><code>AskUserQuestion </code></summary>

<pre><code>User has answered your questions: "The YueScript reference implementation stores timers in @timers array. Should this be changed to add timers as children via @\add_child?"="That's perhaps unfortunate naming, but @timers inside the timer object is each after, every, tween, etc call that's running. Each high-level "timer" can have multiple internal "timers", each being after, every, etc, calls that were called for that high-level timer. Does this make sense? Perhaps we should call the internal ones another name. The internal ones should not be children because that's just how the timer object itself works, in the same way that the box2d body should be a child of the collider object, because the point of the collider is to hide the box2d complexity, not expose it to the tree.", "For physics_get_contact_enter('ball', 'wall'), does tag order matter? Is c.a always the first tag and c.b the second?"="Don't care yet", "Layer composition order - what determines which layer renders on top?"="Creation order by default, can be specified with named order list if needed.", "Build scripts: You're on Linux. Should build.sh handle both Linux and Web, or have separate scripts?"="build.sh for Linux". You can now continue with the user's answers in mind.</code></pre>

</details>

That makes sense! So there are two levels:
- **Timer object** (child of parent) - the high-level timer system
- **timer entries** (inside Timer's `@timers`) - individual `after`/`every`/`tween` calls

I'll suggest renaming the internal array to `@entries` or `@callbacks` to avoid confusion.

<details>
<summary><code>AskUserQuestion </code></summary>

<pre><code>User has answered your questions: "For the internal timer array, what should it be called to avoid confusion with the Timer object itself?"="@callbacks", "all('enemy') searches descendants. If an object has name='enemy' AND tag 'enemy', is it matched once or twice? And can multiple objects have the same name?"="Defer to implementation", "Should rectangle support rounded corners? game:rectangle(x, y, w, h, color, rx, ry)?"="Separate function". You can now continue with the user's answers in mind.</code></pre>

</details>

String: ### Colors

Colors are 32-bit RGBA values (e.g., `0xFF0000FF` for red). A built-in palette is available:

```lua
an.colors.white[0]   -- pure white
an.colors.black[0]   -- pure black
an.colors.red[0]     -- etc.

Palette colors have brightness variants: `[0]` is the base color, negative indices are darker, positive are lighter.&lt;/tool_use_error&gt;</code></pre>

  1326Ôćĺ
  1327ÔćĺDraw order within a layer is submission order (when Lua calls draw functions), not tree order.

  1330Ôćĺ

  1179Ôćĺ```
  1180Ôćĺ
  1181ÔćĺForgetting `super` means no `@children`, `@tags`, etc.
  1182Ôćĺ
  1183Ôćĺ### Special Methods
  1184Ôćĺ
  1185ÔćĺThe engine automatically calls these methods on objects if they exist:
  1186Ôćĺ
  1187Ôćĺ- `early_update(dt)` ÔÇö called during early phase
  1188Ôćĺ- `update(dt)` ÔÇö called during main phase
  1189Ôćĺ- `late_update(dt)` ÔÇö called during late phase (often used for drawing)
  1190Ôćĺ- `destroy()` ÔÇö called when object is removed from tree
  1191Ôćĺ
  1192ÔćĺDefine them as regular class methods:
  1193Ôćĺ```yuescript
  1194Ôćĺclass enemy extends object
  1195Ôćĺ  update: (dt) =&gt;
  1196Ôćĺ    @x, @y = @collider\get_position!
  1197Ôćĺ
  1198Ôćĺ  late_update: (dt) =&gt;
  1199Ôćĺ    game\circle @x, @y, @radius, @color
  1200Ôćĺ```
  1201Ôćĺ
  1202Ôćĺ**Methods and actions both run.** If an object has both an `update:` method and `/` actions, the method runs first, then the actions. This allows class-defined behavior to coexist with dynamically added behavior.
  1203Ôćĺ
  1204Ôćĺ### Colliders and Springs Are Factory Functions
  1205Ôćĺ
  1206ÔćĺColliders and springs are created by factory functions that return objects with specialized functionality:</code></pre>

<summary><code>Edit (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md)</code></summary>

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
  1252Ôćĺ### Layers
  1253Ôćĺ
  1254ÔćĺEach layer is a framebuffer object (FBO). Draw commands are batched per layer:
  1255Ôćĺ
  1256Ôćĺ1. Lua calls `layer:circle()`, `layer:draw_image()`, etc.
  1257Ôćĺ2. Geometry is batched into vertex buffers (batch breaks on texture/shader/blend mode changes)
  1258Ôćĺ3. At end of frame, layers are rendered via draw calls
  1259Ôćĺ4. Post-processing shaders are applied per-layer
  1260Ôćĺ5. Layers composite to screen in creation order (first created = bottom, last = top)
  1261Ôćĺ
  1262ÔćĺLayer order can be overridden if needed:
  1263Ôćĺ```lua
  1264Ôćĺan:set_layer_order({'game', 'effects', 'ui'})
  1265Ôćĺ```
  1266Ôćĺ
  1267Ôćĺ```lua
  1268Ôćĺgame = an:layer('game')
  1269Ôćĺeffects = an:layer('effects')</code></pre>

</details>

All consistency fixes are complete. Here's a summary:

---

## Fixes Applied

**Platform & Build:**
1. **Linux is now a primary platform** (was "eventually") in ANCHOR.md and IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md
2. **Build scripts clarified:** `build.sh` for Linux, `build-web.sh` for Web (Emscripten)
3. **File structure updated** in both docs to reflect the three build scripts

**Colors:**
4. **Removed `[0]` indexing** from all color examples - now just `an.colors.white`
5. **Simplified Colors section** - removed brightness variant documentation

**API Consistency:**
6. **Fixed Drawing example** - `self.body:get_position()` Ôćĺ `self.collider:get_position()`
7. **Added `rounded_rectangle()`** as a separate function in both docs
8. **Added layer order documentation** - creation order by default, with `an:set_layer_order()` override

**Behavior Documentation:**
9. **Documented methods + actions both run** - methods first, then actions
10. **Renamed `@timers` to `@callbacks`** in Timer implementation to avoid confusion with the Timer object itself

---

## Decisions Deferred to Implementation

- Physics collision order (`c.a` vs `c.b` ordering)
- `all()` behavior (name vs tag matching, deduplication)

> Great, any further issues or questions you have?

A few more things I noticed that might be worth clarifying:

<details>

Important clarification! Colliders, springs, and timers should all be tree objects (classes extending object), not factory functions. Let me fix the documentation:

<details>
<summary><code>Edit (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md)</code></summary>

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
  1200Ôćĺ```
  1201Ôćĺ
  1202Ôćĺ**Methods and actions both run.** If an object has both an `update:` method and `/` actions, the method runs first, then the actions. This allows class-defined behavior to coexist with dynamically added behavior.
  1203Ôćĺ
  1204Ôćĺ### Colliders, Springs, and Timers Are Tree Objects
  1205Ôćĺ
  1206ÔćĺColliders, springs, and timers are all proper tree objects (classes extending `object`). They live in `@children` like any other child, and die when their parent dies.
  1207Ôćĺ
  1208Ôćĺ```yuescript
  1209Ôćĺclass collider extends object
  1210Ôćĺ  new: (tag, body_type, shape_type, ...) =&gt;
  1211Ôćĺ    super 'collider'
  1212Ôćĺ    @body_id = an\physics_create_body tag, body_type, shape_type, ...
  1213Ôćĺ
  1214Ôćĺ  get_position: =&gt; an\physics_get_position @body_id
  1215Ôćĺ  set_velocity: (vx, vy) =&gt; an\physics_set_velocity @body_id, vx, vy
  1216Ôćĺ
  1217Ôćĺ  destroy: =&gt;
  1218Ôćĺ    an\physics_destroy_body @body_id
  1219Ôćĺ
  1220Ôćĺ-- Usage: added as child via + operator
  1221Ôćĺ@ + collider 'player', 'dynamic', 'circle', 12
  1222Ôćĺ```
  1223Ôćĺ
  1224ÔćĺThe `destroy` method is called automatically when the collider dies (when parent dies or when explicitly killed).
  1225Ôćĺ
  1226Ôćĺ### Method vs Property Access
  1227Ôćĺ
  1228ÔćĺIn YueScript:</code></pre>

</details>

<details>
<summary><code>Edit (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md)</code></summary>

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
   206Ôćĺ## Lifecycle
   207Ôćĺ
   208Ôćĺ### Update Order
   209Ôćĺ
   210ÔćĺEach frame:
   211Ôćĺ
   212Ôćĺ1. **Collect all objects** ÔÇö Traverse tree, gather all non-dead objects
   213Ôćĺ2. **Early actions** (`%` operator) ÔÇö Run all early_action functions (tree order)
   214Ôćĺ3. **Main actions** (`/` operator) ÔÇö Run all action functions (tree order)
   215Ôćĺ4. **Late actions** (`//` operator) ÔÇö Run all late_action functions (tree order)
   216Ôćĺ5. **Cleanup** ÔÇö Remove dead objects, remove dead actions, call destroy() hooks
   217Ôćĺ
   218ÔćĺObjects created during the frame are collected in the next frame (they don't run actions in the frame they're created).
   219Ôćĺ
   220Ôćĺ### Action Phase Execution</code></pre>

   301Ôćĺ
   302Ôćĺ### Timer
   303Ôćĺ
   304ÔćĺA timer object provides delayed and repeating callbacks, plus tweening. Timer methods (`after`, `every`, `tween`) auto-initialize if needed, so explicit `self:timer()` is optional:
   305Ôćĺ
   306Ôćĺ```lua
   307Ôćĺ-- One-shot (anonymous)
   308Ôćĺself:after(0.5, function() self.can_shoot = true end)
   309Ôćĺ
   310Ôćĺ-- One-shot (named ÔÇö can be cancelled/replaced)
   311Ôćĺself:after(0.15, 'flash', function() self.flashing = false end)
   312Ôćĺ
   313Ôćĺ-- Repeating (anonymous)
   314Ôćĺself:every(2, function() self:spawn_enemy() end)
   315Ôćĺ
   316Ôćĺ-- Repeating (named)
   317Ôćĺself:every(1, 'regen', function() self.hp = self.hp + 1 end)
   318Ôćĺ
   319Ôćĺ-- Cancel a named timer
   320Ôćĺself.flash:kill()
   321Ôćĺ
   322Ôćĺ-- Tween (also provided by timer)
   323Ôćĺself:tween(0.5, self, {x = 100, y = 200}, math.cubic_in_out, function()
   324Ôćĺ    self:kill()
   325Ôćĺend)
   326Ôćĺ```
   327Ôćĺ
   328ÔćĺTimer signature: `after(duration, [name], callback)` ÔÇö the name parameter is optional and appears in the middle position.
   329Ôćĺ
... [5 more lines]</code></pre>

<pre><code>318Ôćĺ
   319Ôćĺ**Objects:** Lua tables in a tree. Have state, update, die. When parent dies, children die immediately.
   320Ôćĺ
   321Ôćĺ### Timers, Springs, Colliders
   322Ôćĺ
   323ÔćĺThese are **child objects**, not mixins. They die when their parent dies. No manual cleanup tracking.
   324Ôćĺ
   325Ôćĺ```lua
   326Ôćĺ-- Child object style
   327Ôćĺself:after(0.5, callback)               -- Timer as action
   328Ôćĺself + spring('main', 1, 200, 10)       -- Spring as child object
   329Ôćĺself + collider('tag', 'dynamic', ...)  -- Collider as child object
   330Ôćĺ```

   317Ôćĺ**Engine (`an`):** C-backed services. Physics, rendering, input, audio, RNG. Always available, don't die.
   318Ôćĺ
   319Ôćĺ**Objects:** Lua tables in a tree. Have state, update, die. When parent dies, children die immediately.
   320Ôćĺ
   321Ôćĺ### Timers, Springs, Colliders
   322Ôćĺ
   323ÔćĺThese are **child objects**, not mixins. They die when their parent dies. No manual cleanup tracking.
   324Ôćĺ
   325Ôćĺ```lua
   326Ôćĺ-- Child object style
   327Ôćĺself + timer()                          -- Timer as child object

   329Ôćĺself + spring('main', 1, 200, 10)       -- Spring as child object

   332Ôćĺ

   638Ôćĺ
   639Ôćĺ### Named Timers Pattern
   640Ôćĺ
   641ÔćĺUse named timers when you need to cancel or replace an ongoing timer:
   642Ôćĺ
   643Ôćĺ```lua
   644Ôćĺfunction enemy:flash()
   645Ôćĺ    self.flashing = true
   646Ôćĺ    self:after(0.15, 'flash', function()
   647Ôćĺ        self.flashing = false
   648Ôćĺ    end)
   649Ôćĺend
   650Ôćĺ
   651Ôćĺ-- Calling flash() again replaces the old timer
   652Ôćĺ-- So rapid hits extend the flash rather than stacking callbacks
   653Ôćĺ
   654Ôćĺfunction enemy:stun(duration)
   655Ôćĺ    -- Cancel any existing stun
   656Ôćĺ    if self.stun_timer then self.stun_timer:kill() end
   657Ôćĺ    
   658Ôćĺ    self.stunned = true
   659Ôćĺ    self:after(duration, 'stun_timer', function()
   660Ôćĺ        self.stunned = false
   661Ôćĺ    end)
   662Ôćĺend
   663Ôćĺ```
   664Ôćĺ</code></pre>

</details>

   551Ôćĺ
   552ÔćĺUse the `+` operator to build object hierarchies declaratively (parent-centric style):
   553Ôćĺ
   554Ôćĺ```lua
   555Ôćĺ-- In constructor
   556Ôćĺfunction arena:new(args)
   557Ôćĺ    self:object('arena', args)

   559Ôćĺ

   561Ôćĺ         + paddle('right', 450, 135)
   562Ôćĺ         + ball()
   563Ôćĺ         + { wall('top', 240, 5),

The main documentation files are now consistent. The `website/context` files will be synced at end of session, and the `yue/*.yue` files are examples from the old L├ľVE-based Anchor (not the new engine).

---

## Summary of All Fixes This Session

**Platform & Build:**
- Linux is now a primary platform (not "eventually")
- Build scripts: `build.sh` (Linux), `build-web.sh` (Web), `build.bat` (Windows)

**Colors:**
- Removed `[0]` indexing - now just `an.colors.white`

**Timer Architecture (major clarification):**
- Timer is a child object class, not a mixin
- Usage: `self + timer()` then `self.timer:after(...)` 
- Named callbacks stored on timer: `self.timer.flash`
- Updated all examples in both ANCHOR.md and CLAUDE.md

**Collider/Spring/Timer Model:**
- All are tree objects (classes extending `object`), not factory functions
- Updated "Colliders and Springs Are Factory Functions" Ôćĺ "Colliders, Springs, and Timers Are Tree Objects"

**Lifecycle:**
- Clarified that cleanup phase removes dead objects AND dead actions
- Documented that methods and actions both run (methods first)

**Layer Order:**
- Creation order by default (first = bottom, last = top)
- Can override with `an:set_layer_order({'game', 'effects', 'ui'})`

**Other:**
- Added `rounded_rectangle()` as separate function
- Fixed Drawing example: `self.body` Ôćĺ `self.collider`
- Renamed internal timer array `@timers` Ôćĺ `@callbacks`
- Removed stale `after`/`every` methods from base object class reference

> Please add the following mention (you may rephrase it freely) in every document where timers, springs, colliders are mentioned: these classes are just examples of how the engine handle "engine" objects, but all "engine" classes work like this, they're individual objects instantiated and added as children to their parents. Importantly, they're all (except in exceptional cases) named after themselves. So when you call @ + timer(), timer() is implicitly created as an object named 'timer', which thus means that the parent can access it via @timer. The same applies to colliders, springs, and all other such engine objects.

   260Ôćĺ---
   261Ôćĺ
   262Ôćĺ## Colliders, Springs, and Timers
   263Ôćĺ
   264ÔćĺEverything in Anchor is an object in the tree. Colliders, springs, and timers are objects with specialized functionality ÔÇö they're added as children just like any other object and die when their parent dies.
   265Ôćĺ
   266ÔćĺThese are examples of **engine objects** ÔÇö classes that wrap C-side resources (physics bodies, audio handles, etc.). All engine objects follow the same pattern:
   267Ôćĺ
   268Ôćĺ1. They're instantiated and added as children: `@ + timer()`
   269Ôćĺ2. They're named after themselves by default, so `timer()` creates an object with `name = 'timer'`
   270Ôćĺ3. The parent can access them via that name: `@timer:after(0.5, fn)`
   271Ôćĺ
   272ÔćĺThis applies to colliders, springs, timers, and any future engine objects.
   273Ôćĺ

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/.claude/CLAUDE.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
   317Ôćĺ**Engine (`an`):** C-backed services. Physics, rendering, input, audio, RNG. Always available, don't die.
   318Ôćĺ
   319Ôćĺ**Objects:** Lua tables in a tree. Have state, update, die. When parent dies, children die immediately.
   320Ôćĺ
   321Ôćĺ### Timers, Springs, Colliders
   322Ôćĺ
   323ÔćĺThese are **engine objects** ÔÇö child objects that wrap C-side resources. They die when their parent dies. No manual cleanup tracking.
   324Ôćĺ
   325ÔćĺEngine objects are named after themselves by default, so `@ + timer()` creates a child named `'timer'`, accessible via `@timer`. This pattern applies to all engine objects (timers, springs, colliders, and any future ones).
   326Ôćĺ
   327Ôćĺ```lua
   328Ôćĺ-- Engine objects as children
   329Ôćĺself + timer()                          -- Creates self.timer
   330Ôćĺself.timer:after(0.5, callback)         -- Access via name
   331Ôćĺself + spring('main', 1, 200, 10)       -- Named 'main', so self.main
   332Ôćĺself + collider('player', 'dynamic', 'circle', 10)  -- Named 'collider', so self.collider
   333Ôćĺ```
   334Ôćĺ
   335Ôćĺ### Layers
   336Ôćĺ

   489Ôćĺ- `after(delay, [name], callback)` ÔÇö one-shot
   490Ôćĺ- `every(interval, [name], callback)` ÔÇö repeating
   491Ôćĺ- `tween(duration, target, props, easing, [callback])` ÔÇö interpolation
   492Ôćĺ- Named timers can be cancelled/replaced

   501Ôćĺ- Wraps Box2D body
   502Ôćĺ- Methods: `get_position`, `set_velocity`, `apply_impulse`, etc.
   503Ôćĺ- Auto-destroys Box2D body on kill

   507Ôćĺ1. Early phase ÔÇö all `early_update` and `%` actions
   508Ôćĺ2. Main phase ÔÇö all `update` and `/` actions
   509Ôćĺ3. Late phase ÔÇö all `late_update` and `//` actions

<details>
<summary><code>Edit (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR_IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md)</code></summary>

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR_IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
   482Ôćĺ- Links: `link` for horizontal dependencies
   483Ôćĺ- Operators: `^`, `%`, `/`, `//`, `+`, `&gt;&gt;`
   484Ôćĺ- `__inherited` hook to propagate operators to child classes
   485Ôćĺ
   486Ôćĺ**Ask developer before implementing details.**
   487Ôćĺ
   488Ôćĺ### Engine Object Pattern
   489Ôćĺ
   490ÔćĺTimer, spring, and collider are examples of **engine objects** ÔÇö classes that wrap C-side resources. All engine objects follow this pattern:
   491Ôćĺ
   492Ôćĺ1. They extend the base `object` class
   493Ôćĺ2. They're instantiated and added as children: `@ + timer()`
   494Ôćĺ3. They're named after themselves by default (e.g., `timer()` has `name = 'timer'`)
   495Ôćĺ4. Parent accesses them via that name: `@timer:after(...)`
   496Ôćĺ5. They implement `destroy()` to clean up C-side resources when killed
   497Ôćĺ
   498ÔćĺThis pattern applies to any future engine objects (particles, audio sources, etc.).
   499Ôćĺ
   500Ôćĺ### 10.3 Timer Object
   501Ôćĺ- `after(delay, [name], callback)` ÔÇö one-shot
   502Ôćĺ- `every(interval, [name], callback)` ÔÇö repeating
   503Ôćĺ- `tween(duration, target, props, easing, [callback])` ÔÇö interpolation
   504Ôćĺ- Named timers can be cancelled/replaced</code></pre>

</details>

   508Ôćĺ- `pull(amount)` ÔÇö displace from target
   509Ôćĺ- `.x` property ÔÇö current value (updated each frame)

   514Ôćĺ- Methods: `get_position`, `set_velocity`, `apply_impulse`, etc.

Done. Added the engine object pattern explanation to all three files:

**ANCHOR.md** (Colliders, Springs, and Timers section):
- Explains engine objects wrap C-side resources
- Lists the 3-step pattern: instantiate Ôćĺ named after themselves Ôćĺ access via name
- Notes this applies to all future engine objects

**CLAUDE.md** (Timers, Springs, Colliders section):
- Same explanation in condensed form
- Updated code example with comments showing the naming

**ANCHOR_IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md** (Phase 10):
- Added "Engine Object Pattern" subsection before Timer/Spring/Collider
- Lists the 5 implementation requirements
- Mentions future objects (particles, audio sources, etc.)

Also removed "factory function" language from Collider and Spring sections since they're proper classes now.

> Great, any parts of the documents that are still inconsistent, wrong, dubious, or that you have questions about?

Yes, I noticed an inconsistency in the engine object naming:

<details>
<summary><code>AskUserQuestion </code></summary>

<pre><code>User has answered your questions: "Engine objects are 'named after themselves by default', but spring('main', ...) takes an explicit name as first arg, while collider('player', ...) uses 'player' as physics tag, not tree name. How should this work?"="This is because the "spring" object was actually multiple springs internally, and the user could then access each one individually. This will be different in new Anchor, as if you want to have multiple springs you just create multiple springs and have them as children. By default, each spring is named "spring", but if you want to have multiple then it can also take its name as an argument. So every time you see spring('main') and it's the only spring, that's wrong, unless the code explicitly wants a spring named 'main', which usually isn't the case since self.spring is way more understandable than self.main. But in cases where there are multiple springs, you'd want @ + spring('attack'), @ + spring('hit') and so on.". You can now continue with the user's answers in mind.</code></pre>

</details>

Got it! Springs (and all engine objects) default to their class name, and only take an explicit name when you need multiple. Let me fix the examples:

<details>
<summary><code>Grep (spring\('main'|spring\('shoot'|spring\('attack')</code></summary>

<pre><code>docs/ANCHOR.md:300:self + spring('main', 1, 200, 10)   -- name, initial, stiffness, damping
docs/ANCHOR.md:301:self + spring('shoot', 1, 300, 15)
docs/ANCHOR.md:360:    self + spring('main', 1, 200, 10)</code></pre>

</details>

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/.claude/CLAUDE.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
   323ÔćĺThese are **engine objects** ÔÇö child objects that wrap C-side resources. They die when their parent dies. No manual cleanup tracking.
   324Ôćĺ
   325ÔćĺEngine objects are named after themselves by default, so `@ + timer()` creates a child named `'timer'`, accessible via `@timer`. This pattern applies to all engine objects (timers, springs, colliders, and any future ones).
   326Ôćĺ
   327Ôćĺ```lua
   328Ôćĺ-- Engine objects as children (default names)
   329Ôćĺself + timer()                          -- Creates self.timer
   330Ôćĺself + spring(1, 200, 10)               -- Creates self.spring
   331Ôćĺself + collider('player', 'dynamic', 'circle', 10)  -- Creates self.collider
   332Ôćĺ
   333Ôćĺ-- Multiple of same type (explicit names)
   334Ôćĺself + spring('attack', 1, 200, 10)     -- Creates self.attack
   335Ôćĺself + spring('hit', 1, 300, 15)        -- Creates self.hit
   336Ôćĺ```
   337Ôćĺ
   338Ôćĺ### Layers
   339Ôćĺ
   340ÔćĺLayers are **engine infrastructure**, created at startup. They're not tree objects. They don't die.</code></pre>

</details>

   754Ôćĺ
   755Ôćĺ*YueScript (evolved from MoonScript) is a language that compiles to Lua. It offers significant syntactic improvements: significant whitespace, `@` for self, `=&gt;` for methods with implicit self, no `end` keywords, `+=`/`-=` operators, and optional parentheses. The operator-based object construction style fits YueScript's aesthetic particularly well.*

Documents are now consistent. Summary of changes this round:

**Engine Object Naming Pattern (all files):**
- All engine objects default to their class name: `timer()` Ôćĺ `self.timer`, `spring()` Ôćĺ `self.spring`, `collider()` Ôćĺ `self.collider`
- Optional explicit name for multiples: `spring('attack', 1, 200, 10)` Ôćĺ `self.attack`
- Updated all Spring examples from `spring('main', ...)` with `self.main` to `spring(...)` with `self.spring` (when single)
- Updated Collider section to show both single (default name) and multiple (explicit name) patterns
- Updated IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md constructors to show `[name]` as optional first parameter

**Other fixes:**
- Removed stale `:timer()` method call from set() chaining example
- Fixed Drawing section to use `self.spring.x` instead of `self.main.x`

---

## Session Summary

**Major changes made:**

1. **Linux as primary platform** - Updated from "eventually" to primary alongside Windows and Web

2. **Build scripts** - `build.sh` (Linux), `build-web.sh` (Web), `build.bat` (Windows)

3. **Timer architecture overhaul** - Timer is now a child object, not a mixin:
   - `self + timer()` then `self.timer:after(...)`
   - Callbacks stored on timer: `self.timer.flash`

4. **Engine object pattern documented** - All engine objects (timer, spring, collider):
   - Default to class name: `timer()` Ôćĺ `self.timer`
   - Optional explicit name for multiples: `spring('attack', ...)` Ôćĺ `self.attack`

5. **Removed "factory function" concept** - All are proper tree objects extending `object`

6. **Spring examples fixed** - Single springs now use `spring(1, 200, 10)` with `self.spring`, not `spring('main', ...)`

7. **Collider naming** - Optional name first, then tag: `collider([name], tag, body_type, shape_type, ...)`

8. **Color format simplified** - `an.colors.white` instead of `an.colors.white[0]`

9. **Methods + actions both run** - Documented that methods run first, then actions

10. **Layer order** - Creation order by default, overridable with `an:set_layer_order()`

11. **Cleanup phase** - Handles dead objects AND dead actions

12. **Renamed internal timer array** - `@timers` Ôćĺ `@callbacks` to avoid confusion

---

>    - Physics collision order: Defer to implementation
>    - Layer order: Creation order by default, can override with named order list
>    - Build scripts: `build.sh` for Linux, separate `build-web.sh` for Web
>    - Rounded rectangles: Separate function `rounded_rectangle()`
> 
> 4. **Major Clarifications from User**:
>    - Timer is a child object with `after`, `every`, `tween` methods
>    - Usage should be `self.timer:after(...)` not `self:after(...)`
>    - All engine objects (timer, spring, collider) should be tree objects, not factory functions
>    - They're named after themselves by default: `timer()` creates `self.timer`
>    - Blend mode behavior: Defer to implementation
>    - Collider/spring/timer are all proper tree objects extending `object`
>    - Dead actions: Separate cleanup pass handles them
> 
> 5. **Engine Object Pattern Clarification**:
>    - User clarified that spring was previously multiple springs internally
>    - New pattern: default name is class name, optional explicit name for multiples
>    - `spring()` Ôćĺ `self.spring`, `spring('attack')` Ôćĺ `self.attack`
>    - Same for timer and collider

After reading all three documents fresh, here's my consistency analysis:

## Issues Found

### 1. `add` vs `add_child` Method Inconsistency

**Examples throughout both docs use `add()`:**
- ANCHOR.md line 151: `parent:add(child)`
- ANCHOR.md line 523: `self:add(timer())`
- CLAUDE.md line 470: `self:add(timer())`

**But the reference implementation only defines `add_child()`:**
- ANCHOR.md lines 969-977 and 1096-1098 show `add_child` as the method name

The `>>` operator implementation uses `add_child`:
```yuescript
__shr: (other) =>
  other\add_child @
  other
```

**This needs reconciliation.** Either:
- Add `add = add_child` alias to the reference implementation, OR
- Change all examples to use `add_child`

Given that examples consistently use `add()`, I'd suggest adding the alias to the reference implementation.

### 2. Minor: Build Function Example Style

In CLAUDE.md lines 467-475, the build function example uses `self:add(timer())`:
```lua
E() ^ function(self)
          self.x, self.y = 100, 200
          self:add(timer())
      end
```

While this works, the `+` operator is the preferred syntax everywhere else. Could be:
```lua
E() ^ function(self)
          self.x, self.y = 100, 200
          self + timer()
      end
```

---

## Everything Else Looks Consistent

- **Engine object default naming** (`timer()` Ôćĺ `self.timer`, `spring(1,200,10)` Ôćĺ `self.spring`, `collider('tag',...)` Ôćĺ `self.collider`) ÔÇö consistent across all three docs
- **Collider signature** `collider([name], tag, body_type, shape_type, ...)` ÔÇö consistent
- **Spring signature** `spring([name], initial, stiffness, damping)` ÔÇö consistent
- **Timer pattern** `self + timer()` then `self.timer:after(...)` ÔÇö consistent, no old `self:after()` patterns remain
- **Color format** `an.colors.white` without `[0]` ÔÇö consistent
- **Platforms** Windows, Linux, Web ÔÇö consistent
- **Build scripts** `build.sh`, `build.bat`, `build-web.sh` ÔÇö consistent
- **Layer order** creation order, overridable via `set_layer_order()` ÔÇö documented
- **Methods + actions both run** ÔÇö documented in ANCHOR.md
- **Cleanup removes dead actions** ÔÇö documented in update order
- **`rounded_rectangle()` function** ÔÇö in both ANCHOR.md and IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md

---

Would you like me to fix the `add`/`add_child` inconsistency by adding an alias to the reference implementation?

> Alright, given everything you now know about the engine, and given what's in the actual documents, including the context brief about *why* these decisions were made, what's your opinion on Anchor as an engine? Do the stated philosophical principles actually play out in the actual usage of the engine? Does it seem like it's a good engine to make games with? Does it feel elegant? Etc. Give me your full opinion. This is essentially the same consistency check, except at a higher, more soft level of analysis. ultrathink

~~~
This is a great question. Let me think through this carefully.

## Does the Philosophy Actually Manifest?

### Locality - Yes, Genuinely Achieved

The operator syntax delivers on its promise. This:

```yuescript
E 'ball' ^ {x: 240, y: 135, vx: 100, vy: 100}
         / (dt) =>
             @x += @vx * dt
             game\circle @x, @y, 8, an.colors.white
         >> arena
```

...genuinely contains everything about the ball in one expression: name, initial state, behavior, tree position. Compare to Unity (separate script file, prefab, inspector values, spawning code) or even plain L├ľVE (class definition, update function, draw function, adding to a list). Anchor collapses all of that.

The `>>` operator is particularly well-chosen. "Flow this object into that parent" reads left-to-right naturally. The whole pipeline (`create Ôćĺ configure Ôćĺ add behavior Ôćĺ place in tree`) is visible in reading order.

### No Bureaucracy - Yes, Actually Achieved

The globals being "just there" (`an`, `E`, `X`, `A`) is genuinely liberating. No imports, no registration, no dependency injection ceremony.

The engine object pattern is elegant:
- `self + timer()` and it exists as `self.timer`
- No manual cleanup ever
- No "remember to destroy this in your destructor"

The default naming convention (`timer()` Ôćĺ `self.timer`) is a small touch that eliminates boilerplate without feeling magical. You know what it does, it just saves you typing `self.timer = timer()`.

### Tree-Based Ownership - The Core Insight

This is the most elegant part of the design. The observation that "parent dies Ôćĺ children die" captures a huge percentage of real game relationships:
- Player dies Ôćĺ weapons, effects, UI indicators die
- Enemy dies Ôćĺ health bar, AI state, particles die  
- Arena/room dies Ôćĺ everything in it dies

And the `link()` escape hatch for horizontal dependencies (siblings, cousins) handles the cases where tree structure doesn't fit, with clear guidance on when to use each.

The named child replacement behavior (adding `'ball'` kills the existing `'ball'`) is subtle but powerful ÔÇö it handles "respawn" patterns without explicit cleanup code.

### Alien But Right - Mostly, With Reservations

The syntax earns most of its complexity. Once internalized, `E 'ball' ^ {...} / fn >> arena` is faster to read and write than verbose alternatives.

However, some operator choices feel arbitrary:

- **`//` for late_action** ÔÇö In most languages `//` is a comment or floor division. YueScript uses `--` for comments, so there's no conflict, but programmers from other backgrounds will do a double-take. It's also visually noisy.

- **`%` for early_action** ÔÇö There's no mnemonic. `^` suggests "power up/initialize", `/` suggests "action/do something", but `%` for "before"? It's just... chosen.

- **The `X` helper** ÔÇö `X('name', fn)` returning `{[name]: fn}` which the operator detects and unpacks is clever but magical. The method alternative (`self:action('name', fn)`) is more verbose but more obvious. The `X` works, but it's a layer of indirection you have to internalize.

These are minor quibbles. The overall syntax does earn its weirdness.

## Tensions and Potential Issues

### 1. Collision Handling Breaks Locality

The documented pattern puts collision logic in arena:

```lua
function arena:update(dt)
    for _, c in ipairs(an:physics_get_contact_enter('player', 'enemy')) do
        local player, enemy = c.a, c.b
        player:take_damage(enemy.damage)
    end
end
```

This is rules-based collision handling. The player's response to hitting an enemy is defined in arena, not in player. 

The documentation acknowledges this ("rules-based code can live inside objects when it's self-contained"), and the rationale is sound (Box2D event queries are cleaner than callback hell). But it does mean collision-related behavior is scattered.

This isn't a flaw ÔÇö it's a conscious tradeoff. But it's worth noting that locality has limits, and collision handling is where those limits appear.

### 2. Draw Order vs Tree Order

Draw order is submission order, not tree order. This is the right choice (flexibility), but it means the visual layering of objects isn't expressed where the objects are defined.

If you want all enemies behind all players, you either:
- Use different layers
- Carefully order your draw calls
- Accept that tree structure doesn't help here

Again, conscious tradeoff, correctly made. But locality doesn't extend to visual ordering.

### 3. Timer/Spring/Collider Unification

These are all "engine objects" with the same pattern, but they feel conceptually different:

- **Timer** ÔÇö purely temporal, no spatial component
- **Spring** ÔÇö animation/interpolation, has a value that changes  
- **Collider** ÔÇö physics presence, has position/velocity/forces

Grouping them reduces cognitive load ("they all work the same way"), but the abstraction papers over real differences. A timer is a "capability" you add; a collider is a "physical presence." These feel different even if they're implemented similarly.

### 4. YueScript Lock-in

The engine is deeply tied to YueScript: operator overloading, `@` for self, the class system. Plain Lua users can't use the nice syntax at all.

This is fine ÔÇö the developer chose YueScript deliberately for its aesthetics. But it means:
- Any YueScript bugs become engine limitations
- The `-r` flag is critical (without it, errors point to wrong lines)
- Development is YueScript-or-bust

Build-time compilation mitigates some risk (no runtime YueScript dependency), but the ergonomics assume YueScript.

### 5. The Reference Implementation's Status

The YueScript reference implementation in ANCHOR.md shows how the object system works internally. But it's unclear if this is:
- Prescriptive (implement exactly this)
- Illustrative (shows the concept, C implementation may differ)

For a C engine with Lua scripting, the actual object system will be partially in C. The reference implementation is useful for understanding, but the relationship to final implementation is fuzzy.

## What's Genuinely Elegant

1. **The tree model** ÔÇö Simple, powerful, eliminates orphaned objects and manual cleanup entirely

2. **Engine objects as children** ÔÇö Timer, spring, collider becoming tree nodes with automatic lifecycle is clever and consistent

3. **The operator pipeline** ÔÇö `E() ^ {...} / fn >> parent` is genuinely expressive

4. **Default naming** ÔÇö `timer()` Ôćĺ `self.timer` without explicit assignment

5. **Named child replacement** ÔÇö Adding a child with an existing name kills the old one automatically

6. **Methods + actions both run** ÔÇö Class-defined behavior coexists with dynamically added behavior cleanly

## What Could Be Better

1. **Operator choices** ÔÇö `//` and `%` feel arbitrary; better mnemonics would help

2. **Collision handling location** ÔÇö Necessary tradeoff but breaks locality

3. **The `X` helper** ÔÇö Works but feels like a magic escape hatch

## Is It Good for Making Games?

**Yes, for its intended use case.**

Anchor is designed for:
- Solo developers
- Action games (physics-heavy, juicy, lots of objects)
- Small-to-medium scope
- Developers who value code aesthetics

For this profile, it looks excellent. The locality principle serves solo workflow. The tree model handles 90% of object lifetime. The terse syntax reduces friction.

It would NOT be good for:
- Large teams (globals and implicit behavior cause coordination problems)
- Non-YueScript users (syntax advantages disappear)
- Games with complex many-to-many relationships
- Developers who prefer explicit over implicit

## Does It Feel Elegant?

**Mostly yes.**

The elegance comes from consistent application of a coherent vision. The tree model, the operator syntax, the engine object pattern ÔÇö they all reinforce each other. The stated principles (locality, no bureaucracy, tree ownership) are actually reflected in the API, not just marketing.

The test of elegance: does the complexity serve a purpose? For most of Anchor's unusual choices, yes. The weirdness buys terseness and expressiveness. A few choices (`//`, `%`, `X`) feel like they could be refined, but they're not deal-breakers.

## The Deeper Question

Reading the Context Brief, I notice the developer works "primarily by intuition ÔÇö choices are made because they 'feel right' before rational justification."

This shows in the design. Anchor *feels* like a coherent artifact, not a committee-designed API. The operator syntax, the single-letter aliases, the tree model ÔÇö they have a personality. Someone with strong aesthetic opinions made these choices.

The risk with intuition-driven design is inconsistency or blind spots. But having read all three documents, I don't see that here. The design is consistent. The tradeoffs are acknowledged. The escape hatches exist where they need to.

The real test is whether it feels right when actually making a game. Theory only goes so far. But the design is sound enough that I'd expect the feel to follow ÔÇö especially since the developer has shipped games before and knows what friction feels like in practice.

**Final assessment:** Anchor is a well-designed engine that genuinely embodies its stated philosophy. The tree model is the core insight, and everything else supports it coherently. The syntax is unusual but earns its complexity. For a solo developer making action games who values code aesthetics, this looks like a genuinely good tool.
~~~

> Let's handle each issue individually, what should // and % be changed to? Remember that we are bound to Lua/YueScript operators, and that their order matters since operators have specific ways in which they apply (without having to apply parenthesis).

~~~
Good question. Let me look at what's actually available.

**Lua/YueScript operators at the same precedence level as `/`:**
- `*` (multiply)
- `/` (divide) ÔÇö currently main action
- `//` (floor divide) ÔÇö currently late_action
- `%` (modulo) ÔÇö currently early_action

These all chain left-to-right naturally, which is why the current setup works without parentheses.

**The problem:**
- `%` has no mnemonic for "early"
- `//` has no mnemonic for "late" (and looks like comments in other languages)

**A better assignment:**

| Operator | Phase | Mnemonic |
|----------|-------|----------|
| `*` | early_action | Asterisk/star ÔÇö prominent, foundational, "prepare" |
| `/` | action | Slash ÔÇö the main action (unchanged) |
| `%` | late_action | Modulo/remainder ÔÇö "what's left to do after" |

The `%` Ôćĺ late connection is actually decent: modulo gives you the *remainder* after division. Late actions are what *remain* to be done after main processing (often drawing, which needs final positions).

The `*` Ôćĺ early connection is weaker but workable: asterisks mark importance, suggest "everything" (wildcard), visually more prominent than `/`.

The visual progression `*` Ôćĺ `/` Ôćĺ `%` also has coherence ÔÇö they're all multiplicative-family operators.

```yuescript
E() ^ {x: 100}
   * (dt) => -- early: physics prep
   / (dt) => -- main: logic
   % (dt) => -- late: drawing
   >> arena
```

This frees `//` entirely ÔÇö we just don't use it. One less operator to learn.

**Alternative: keep `//` as an alias for `%`?** Both have the same precedence, both could mean "late." But that's probably more confusing than helpful ÔÇö better to have one canonical way.

What do you think of `*` / `/` / `%` for early / main / late?
~~~

> Is there a way we could have all actions use / but with some kind of modifier for each?

~~~
Interesting idea. Since `/` already detects the `X('name', fn)` pattern (a table with a single string key mapping to a function), we could extend that detection for phase markers.

**Option 1: Full-word helpers**
```yuescript
/ before (dt) => ...    -- early
/ (dt) => ...           -- main
/ after (dt) => ...     -- late
```

Where `before` and `after` are functions that wrap the callback in a detectable table. Clear, but longer.

**Option 2: Single-letter helpers**
```yuescript
/ B (dt) => ...    -- Before (early)
/ (dt) => ...      -- main
/ L (dt) => ...    -- Late
```

`B` breaks the runic alphabet rule (it's round), but the mnemonic is strong. `L` is in the runic set and works.

**Option 3: Runic-only (weaker mnemonics)**
```yuescript
/ U (dt) => ...    -- Under/underlying (early, foundational)
/ (dt) => ...      -- main
/ L (dt) => ...    -- Late
```

`U` is runic and "underlying" sort of works for "foundational stuff that runs first."

**Option 4: Prefix convention with X**
```yuescript
/ X '<', (dt) => ...    -- < means before (early)
/ (dt) => ...           -- main
/ X '>', (dt) => ...    -- > means after (late)
```

Reuses X, uses visual arrows. Named versions: `X '<physics', fn`, `X '>draw', fn`.

---

For named actions in options 1-3, we'd need combined helpers or a signature extension:
```yuescript
/ before 'physics', (dt) => ...   -- named early
/ X 'update', (dt) => ...         -- named main
/ after 'draw', (dt) => ...       -- named late
```

Which of these directions feels right to you? Or should I explore something else?
~~~

> Can you show me a few complete code examples using U, L and X '<' X '>'? So I can see what it looks like. Actually, create a .yue file in the yue/ folder so I can open it on my editor.

<details>
<summary><code>Write (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/yue/operator_comparison.yue)</code></summary>

<pre><code>File created successfully at: /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/yue/operator_comparison.yue</code></pre>

</details>

Created `/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/yue/operator_comparison.yue`. 

Key observations from writing these out:

**U/L approach:**
- Anonymous early/late are very clean: `/ U (dt) =>`, `/ L (dt) =>`
- Named versions need separate helpers: `/ U 'physics', (dt) =>`, `/ L 'draw', (dt) =>`
- Introduces two new single-letter globals (U and L)

**X prefix approach:**
- Anonymous early/late require X: `/ X '<', (dt) =>` ÔÇö more verbose
- Named versions are compact: `/ X '<physics', (dt) =>` ÔÇö the prefix is part of the name
- Reuses existing X helper, no new globals
- The `<` and `>` have natural "before/after" meaning (arrows pointing left/right on a timeline)

The tradeoff is basically:
- **U/L**: Cleaner for anonymous actions, but adds two globals
- **X prefix**: More verbose for anonymous, but unified under one mechanism

> We could use E for early and another letter for object itself. What do you think?

~~~
Interesting idea. If E becomes Early, we need a new letter for object. From the runic set (E, X, A, T, L, V, U, Y, I, H):

| Letter | Mnemonic | Feel |
|--------|----------|------|
| **O** | Object | Obvious, but breaks runic rule (round) |
| **T** | Thing | Casual, clear, runic |
| **U** | Unit | Game-y, runic |
| **I** | Instance | Technical, runic |

How they'd read:

```yuescript
-- O for Object (breaks runic, but immediately obvious)
O 'ball' ^ {x: 100}
   / E (dt) => -- early
   / (dt) => -- main
   / L (dt) => -- late
   >> arena

-- T for Thing (runic, casual)
T 'ball' ^ {x: 100}
   / E (dt) =>
   / (dt) =>
   / L (dt) =>
   >> arena
```

**My take:**

`O` for Object is the most intuitive ÔÇö zero learning curve. Breaking the runic rule might be worth it for the most frequently used alias.

`T` for Thing is the best runic option. "Thing" is how we casually refer to game objects anyway ("spawn a thing", "kill that thing"). It's unpretentious.

`U` and `I` feel like stretches.

The E/L pairing for Early/Late is strong ÔÇö clear mnemonics, both runic. The question is whether `O` or `T` feels right for object creation.

Want me to update the comparison file with O and T variants so you can see them in your editor?
~~~

   184Ôćĺ--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   185Ôćĺ-- THE FULL ALIAS SET
   186Ôćĺ--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   187Ôćĺ
   188Ôćĺ-- T = object constructor (mnemonic: Thing)
   189Ôćĺ-- E = early action (mnemonic: Early)
   190Ôćĺ-- X = named action (mnemonic: eXplicit)
   191Ôćĺ-- L = late action (mnemonic: Late)
   192Ôćĺ-- A = all() query (mnemonic: All)
   193Ôćĺ</code></pre>

</details>

Done. Now using:
- **T** = object constructor (Thing)
- **E** = early action (Early)
- **L** = late action (Late)
- **X** = named action (eXplicit)
- **A** = all() query (All)

T for "Thing" has a decent mnemonic ÔÇö it's casual and how we often refer to game objects informally. `T 'ball'` reads as "Thing named ball" or just "a thing: ball".

> Let's try X as object, and for named actions you can choose for now.

<details>
<summary><code>Edit (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/yue/operator_comparison.yue)</code></summary>

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/yue/operator_comparison.yue has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
   184Ôćĺ--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   185Ôćĺ-- THE FULL ALIAS SET
   186Ôćĺ--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   187Ôćĺ
   188Ôćĺ-- X = object constructor
   189Ôćĺ-- E = early action (mnemonic: Early)
   190Ôćĺ-- N = named action (mnemonic: Named) -- breaks runic rule but clear mnemonic
   191Ôćĺ-- L = late action (mnemonic: Late)
   192Ôćĺ-- A = all() query (mnemonic: All)
   193Ôćĺ</code></pre>

</details>

Done. Now using:
- **X** = object constructor
- **E** = early action (Early)
- **N** = named action (Named) ÔÇö breaks runic but strongest mnemonic
- **L** = late action (Late)
- **A** = all() query (All)

X for object has no obvious mnemonic, but it's visually striking and the most "runic" looking letter. N for Named is clear even though it breaks the angular rule.

Alternative for N if you want to stay runic: **T** for "Tagged" ÔÇö the name acts like a tag for the action.

> Nah, let's settle on E for object, U for early, L for late, X for named, A for all.

| Alias | Purpose | Mnemonic |
|-------|---------|----------|
| **E** | object | Entity |

| **L** | late action | Late |
| **X** | named action | eXplicit |
| **A** | all() query | All |

All runic, all with decent mnemonics. U for "under/underlying" captures the idea of foundational work that runs beneath the main logic.

This is a large file. I'll make the changes systematically. The key conceptual shift is that instead of separate operators (`%`, `/`, `//`), we now use `/` for all actions with `U` and `L` helpers to mark early/late phases.

<details>
<summary><code>Edit (/home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md)</code></summary>

<pre><code>The file /home/adn/a327ex/Anchor/docs/ANCHOR.md has been updated. Here's the result of running `cat -n` on a snippet of the edited file:
    80Ôćĺ
    81ÔćĺThese patterns have their place ÔÇö large teams need boundaries, enterprise software needs flexibility. But for solo development, they're pure friction. Every layer of indirection is a place where bugs can hide and a concept you must hold in your head.
    82Ôćĺ
    83ÔćĺAnchor actively rejects these patterns. Instead:
    84Ôćĺ- **Globals are available:** `an`, `E`, `X`, `U`, `L`, `A` are just there. No imports needed. You can use them from anywhere, in any file, without setup.
    85Ôćĺ- **Direct mutation:** Set properties directly. `self.hp = 100`. No setter methods, no signals, no observers pattern. If you want to react to a change, you check the value in your update function.
    86Ôćĺ- **Explicit calls:** When something happens, you call a function. `enemy:take_damage(10)`. No event buses, no implicit subscriptions, no wondering "what else triggers when this happens?"
    87Ôćĺ- **Zero setup:** Drop a `.ttf` in a folder and it loads. Create an object and it exists. The engine assumes you want things to work, not that you want to configure how they work.
    88Ôćĺ</code></pre>

</details>
